class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Thermodynamics_sidebar" title="Template:Thermodynamics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Thermodynamics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Thermodynamics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Thermodynamics_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Body_Temp_Variation.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="357" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Body_Temp_Variation.png/300px-Body_Temp_Variation.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Body_Temp_Variation.png/450px-Body_Temp_Variation.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/05/Body_Temp_Variation.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Body_Temp_Variation.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Daily variation in human body temperature</div></div></div>\n<p><b>Temperature</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Physical_quantity" title="Physical quantity">physical quantity</a> expressing hot and cold. It is <a href="/wiki/Measurement" title="Measurement">measured</a> with a <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometer</a> <a href="/wiki/Calibration" title="Calibration">calibrated</a> in one or more <a href="/wiki/Conversion_of_units_of_temperature" title="Conversion of units of temperature">temperature scales</a>.  The most commonly used scales are the <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">Celsius scale</a> (formerly called <i>centigrade</i>) (denoted &#176;C), <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">Fahrenheit scale</a> (denoted &#176;F), and <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">Kelvin scale</a> (denoted K). The kelvin (the word is spelled with a <a href="/wiki/Letter_case" title="Letter case">lower-case</a> k) is the unit of temperature in the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> (SI), in which temperature is one of the seven fundamental <a href="/wiki/SI_base_unit" title="SI base unit">base quantities</a>. The Kelvin scale is widely used in <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a>.\n</p><p>Theoretically, the coldest a system can be is when its temperature is <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a>, at which point the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_theory_of_gases" title="Kinetic theory of gases">thermal motion</a> in matter would be zero. However, an actual physical system or object can never attain a temperature of absolute zero. Absolute zero is denoted as <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>0&#160;K</span> on the Kelvin scale, <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>&#8722;273.15&#160;&#176;C</span> on the Celsius scale, and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>&#8722;459.67&#160;&#176;F</span> on the Fahrenheit scale.\n</p><p>For an <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">ideal gas</a>, temperature is proportional to the average kinetic energy of the random microscopic motions of the constituent microscopic particles.\n</p><p>Temperature is important in all fields of <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural science</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">Earth science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Medicine" title="Medicine">medicine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, as well as most aspects of daily life.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div physical processes are affected by temperature, such as\n</p>\n<ul><li>physical properties of materials including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phases_of_matter" title="Phases of matter">phase</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solid" title="Solid">solid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liquid" title="Liquid">liquid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gas" title="Gas">gaseous</a> or <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal radiation</a> emitted from the surface of an object</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Speed_of_sound" title="Speed of sound">speed of sound</a> is a function of the square root of the absolute temperature<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" Scales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Scale_of_temperature" title="Scale of temperature">Scale of temperature</a></div>\n<table class="box-Refimprove_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Temperature%22" rel="nofollow">"Temperature"</a>&#160;&#8211;&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Temperature%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">news</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Temperature%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Temperature%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Temperature%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Temperature%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2012</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Temperature scales differ in two ways: the point chosen as zero degrees, and the magnitudes of incremental units or degrees on the scale.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">Celsius</a> scale (&#176;C) is used for common temperature measurements in most of the world. It is an empirical scale that was developed by a historical progress, which led to its zero point <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273149999999999&#9824;</span>0&#160;&#176;C</span> being defined by the freezing point of water, and additional degrees defined so that <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002373150000000000&#9824;</span>100&#160;&#176;C</span> was the boiling point of water, both at sea-level atmospheric pressure. Because of the 100-degree interval, it was called a centigrade scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Since the standardization of the kelvin in the International System of Units, it has subsequently been redefined in terms of the equivalent fixing points on the Kelvin scale, and so that a temperature increment of one degree Celsius is the same as an increment of one kelvin, though they differ by an additive offset of 273.15.\n</p><p>The United States commonly uses the <a href="/wiki/Fahrenheit" title="Fahrenheit">Fahrenheit</a> scale, on which water freezes at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273149999999999&#9824;</span>32&#160;&#176;F</span> and boils at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002373150000000000&#9824;</span>212&#160;&#176;F</span> at sea-level atmospheric pressure.\n</p><p>Many scientific measurements use the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kelvin_temperature_scale" title="Kelvin temperature scale">Kelvin temperature scale</a> (unit symbol: K), named in honor of the Scots-Irish physicist who first defined it. It is a thermodynamic or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_temperature" title="Absolute temperature">absolute temperature</a> scale. Its zero point, <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>0&#160;K</span>, is defined to coincide with the coldest physically-possible temperature (called <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a>). Its degrees are defined <a href="#Definition_of_the_Kelvin_scale">through thermodynamics</a>.  The temperature of absolute zero occurs at  <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>0&#160;K</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>&#8722;273.15&#160;&#176;C</span> (or <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>&#8722;459.67&#160;&#176;F</span>), and the freezing point of water at sea-level atmospheric pressure occurs at <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273149999999999&#9824;</span>273.15&#160;K</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273149999999999&#9824;</span>0&#160;&#176;C</span>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> (SI) defines a scale and unit for the kelvin or <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">thermodynamic temperature</a> by using the reliably reproducible temperature of the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> of water as a second reference point (the first reference point being <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>0&#160;K</span> at absolute zero). The triple point is a singular state with its own unique and invariant temperature and pressure, along with, for a fixed mass of water in a vessel of fixed volume, an autonomically and stably self-determining partition into three mutually contacting phases, vapour, liquid, and solid, dynamically depending only on the total internal energy of the mass of water. For historical reasons, the triple point temperature of water is fixed at 273.16 units of the measurement increment.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>There is a variety of kinds of temperature scale. It may be convenient to classify them as empirically and theoretically based. Empirical temperature scales are historically older, while theoretically based scales arose in the middle of the nineteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truesdell_1980_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Truesdell_1980-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Empirically-based">Empirically-based</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Empirically-based">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Empirically based temperature scales rely directly on measurements of simple physical properties of materials. For example, the length of a column of mercury, confined in a glass-walled capillary tube, is dependent largely on temperature, and is the basis of the very useful mercury-in-glass thermometer. Such scales are valid only within convenient ranges of temperature. For example, above the boiling point of mercury, a mercury-in-glass thermometer is impracticable. Most materials expand with temperature increase, but some materials, such as water, contract with temperature increase over some specific range, and then they are hardly useful as thermometric materials. A material is of no use as a thermometer near one of its phase-change temperatures, for example its boiling-point.\n</p><p>In spite of these restrictions, most generally used practical thermometers are of the empirically based kind. Especially, it was used for <a href="/wiki/Calorimetry" title="Calorimetry">calorimetry</a>, which contributed greatly to the discovery of thermodynamics. Nevertheless, empirical thermometry has serious drawbacks when judged as a basis for theoretical physics. Empirically based thermometers, beyond their base as simple direct measurements of ordinary physical properties of thermometric materials, can be re-calibrated, by use of theoretical physical reasoning, and this can extend their range of adequacy.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Theoretically-based">Theoretically-based</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Theoretically-based">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Theoretically-based temperature scales are based directly on theoretical arguments, especially those of thermodynamics, kinetic theory and quantum mechanics. They rely on theoretical properties of idealized devices and materials. They are more or less comparable with practically feasible physical devices and materials. Theoretically based temperature scales are used to provide calibrating standards for practical empirically based thermometers.\n</p><p>The accepted fundamental thermodynamic temperature scale is the Kelvin scale, based on an ideal cyclic process envisaged for a <a href="/wiki/Carnot_heat_engine" title="Carnot heat engine">Carnot heat engine</a>.\n</p><p>An ideal material on which a temperature scale can be based is the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">ideal gas</a>. The pressure exerted by a fixed volume and mass of an ideal gas is directly proportional to its temperature. Some natural gases show so nearly ideal properties over suitable temperature ranges that they can be used for thermometry; this was important during the development of thermodynamics and is still of practical importance today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The ideal gas thermometer is, however, not theoretically perfect for thermodynamics. This is because the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas#Entropy" title="Ideal gas">entropy of an ideal gas</a> at its absolute zero of temperature is not a positive semi-definite quantity, which puts the gas in violation of the third law of thermodynamics. The physical reason is that the ideal gas law, exactly read, refers to the limit of infinitely high temperature and zero pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Measurement of the spectrum of electromagnetic radiation from an ideal three-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Black_body" title="Black body">black body</a> can provide an accurate temperature measurement because the frequency of maximum spectral radiance of black-body radiation is directly proportional to the temperature of the black body; this is known as <a href="/wiki/Wien%27s_displacement_law" title="Wien\'s displacement law">Wien\'s displacement law</a> and has a theoretical explanation in <a href="/wiki/Planck%27s_law" title="Planck\'s law">Planck\'s law</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_statistics" title="Bose&#8211;Einstein statistics">Bose&#8211;Einstein law</a>.\n</p><p>Measurement of the spectrum of noise-power produced by an electrical resistor can also provide an accurate temperature measurement. The resistor has two terminals and is in effect a one-dimensional body. The Bose-Einstein law for this case indicates that the noise-power is directly proportional to the temperature of the resistor and to the value of its resistance and to the noise band-width. In a given frequency band, the noise-power has equal contributions from every frequency and is called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Johnson_noise" title="Johnson noise">Johnson noise</a>. If the value of the resistance is known then the temperature can be found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p><p>If molecules, or atoms, or electrons,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> are emitted from a material and their velocities are measured, the spectrum of their velocities often nearly obeys a theoretical law called the <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%E2%80%93Boltzmann_distribution" title="Maxwell&#8211;Boltzmann distribution">Maxwell&#8211;Boltzmann distribution</a>, which gives a well-founded measurement of temperatures for which the law holds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> There have not yet been successful experiments of this same kind that directly use the <a href="/wiki/Fermi%E2%80%93Dirac_statistics" title="Fermi&#8211;Dirac statistics">Fermi&#8211;Dirac distribution</a> for thermometry, but perhaps that will be achieved in future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermodynamic_approach">Thermodynamic approach</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Thermodynamic approach">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Temperature is one of the principal quantities in the study of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kelvin_scale_and_absolute_thermodynamic_definitions">Kelvin scale and absolute thermodynamic definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Kelvin scale and absolute thermodynamic definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kelvin_scale" title="Kelvin scale">Kelvin scale</a> is called <b>absolute</b> for two reasons. One is that its formal character is independent of the properties of particular materials. The other reason is that its zero is in a sense absolute, in that it indicates absence of microscopic classical motion of the constituent particles of matter, so that they have a limiting specific heat of zero for zero temperature, according to the third law of thermodynamics. Nevertheless, a Kelvin temperature does in fact have a definite numerical value that has been arbitrarily chosen by tradition and is dependent on the property of a particular materials; it is simply less arbitrary than relative "degrees" scales such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celsius_scale" title="Celsius scale">Celsius</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fahrenheit_scale" title="Fahrenheit scale">Fahrenheit</a>.  Being an absolute scale with one fixed point (zero), there is only one degree of freedom left to arbitrary choice, rather than two as in relative scales. For the Kelvin scale in modern times, this choice of convention is made to be that of setting the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point#Triple_points_of_water" title="Triple point">gas&#8211;liquid&#8211;solid triple point of water</a>, a point which can be reliably reproduced as a standard experimental phenomenon, at a numerical value of 273.16 kelvins. The Kelvin scale is also called the <b>thermodynamic scale</b>. However, to demonstrate that its numerical value is indeed arbitrary, it is useful to point out that an alternate, less widely used absolute temperature scale exists called the <a href="/wiki/Rankine_scale" title="Rankine scale">Rankine scale</a>, made to be aligned with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fahrenheit_scale" title="Fahrenheit scale">Fahrenheit scale</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kelvin_scale" title="Kelvin scale">Kelvin</a> is with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celsius_scale" title="Celsius scale">Celsius</a>.\n</p><p>The thermodynamic definition of temperature is due to Kelvin.\n</p><p>It is framed in terms of an idealized device called a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carnot_engine" title="Carnot engine">Carnot engine</a>, imagined to define a continuous cycle of states of its working body. The cycle is imagined to run so slowly that at each point of the cycle the working body is in a state of thermodynamic equilibrium. There are four limbs in such a <a href="/wiki/Carnot_cycle" title="Carnot cycle">Carnot cycle</a>. The engine consists of four bodies. The main one is called the working body. Two of them are called heat reservoirs, so large that their respective non-deformation variables are not changed by transfer of energy as heat through a wall permeable only to heat to the working body. The fourth body is able to exchange energy with the working body only through adiabatic work; it may be called the work reservoir. The substances and states of the two heat reservoirs should be chosen so that they are not in thermal equilibrium with one another. This means that they must be at different fixed temperatures, one, labeled here with the number 1, hotter than the other, labeled here with the number 2. This can be tested by connecting the heat reservoirs successively to an auxiliary empirical thermometric body that starts each time at a convenient fixed intermediate temperature. The thermometric body should be composed of a material that has a strictly monotonic relation between its chosen empirical thermometric variable and the amount of adiabatic isochoric work done on it. In order to settle the structure and sense of operation of the Carnot cycle, it is convenient to use such a material also for the working body; because most materials are of this kind, this is hardly a restriction of the generality of this definition. The Carnot cycle is considered to start from an initial condition of the working body that was reached by the completion of a reversible adiabatic compression. From there, the working body is initially connected by a wall permeable only to heat to the heat reservoir number 1, so that during the first limb of the cycle it expands and does work on the work reservoir. The second limb of the cycle sees the working body expand adiabatically and reversibly, with no energy exchanged as heat, but more energy being transferred as work to the work reservoir. The third limb of the cycle sees the working body connected, through a wall permeable only to heat, to the heat reservoir 2, contracting and accepting energy as work from the work reservoir. The cycle is closed by reversible adiabatic compression of the working body, with no energy transferred as heat, but energy being transferred to it as work from the work reservoir.\n</p><p>With this set-up, the four limbs of the reversible <a href="/wiki/Carnot_cycle" title="Carnot cycle">Carnot cycle</a> are characterized by amounts of energy transferred, as work from the working body to the work reservoir, and as heat from the heat reservoirs to the working body. The amounts of energy transferred as heat from the heat reservoirs are measured through the changes in the non-deformation variable of the working body, with reference to the previously known properties of that body, the amounts of work done on the work reservoir, and the first law of thermodynamics. The amounts of energy transferred as heat respectively from reservoir 1 and from reservoir 2 may then be denoted respectively <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>1</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>2</sub></span>. Then the absolute or thermodynamic temperatures, <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i><sub>1</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i><sub>2</sub></span>,  of the reservoirs are defined so that to be such that\n</p>\n<dl><dd>\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse; background:none; margin:0; border:none;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="nowrap" style="vertical-align:middle; border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0;"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle T_{1}/T_{2}=-Q_{1}/Q_{2}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>Q</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<msub>\n<mi>Q</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>2</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle T_{1}/T_{2}=-Q_{1}/Q_{2}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle T_{1}/T_{2}=-Q_{1}/Q_{2}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4a800ec630e698f51d01fce41fb2f9c32e780196" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:18.487ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span></p>\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align:middle; width:99%; border:none; padding:0.08em;">\n<div style="margin:0;">\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse; background:none; margin:0; border:none; width:99%;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td rowspan="2" style="border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0; font-size:4pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td>\n<td style="width:100%; border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0; font-size:1pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0; font-size:4pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="border-left:none; border-top:0px none #e5e5e5; border-right:none; border-bottom:none; padding:0.08em;">\n<p style="margin:0; font-size:1pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n</td>\n<td class="nowrap" style="vertical-align:middle; border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0pt;"><b>(1)</b></p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>\n<p>Kelvin\'s original work postulating absolute temperature was published in 1848. It was based on the work of Carnot, before the formulation of the first law of thermodynamics. Kelvin wrote in his 1848 paper that his scale was absolute in the sense that it was defined "independently of the properties of any particular kind of matter". His definitive publication, which sets out the definition just stated, was printed in 1853, a paper read in 1851.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>This definition rests on the physical assumption that there are readily available walls permeable only to heat. In his detailed definition of a wall permeable only to heat, <a href="/wiki/Constantin_Carath%C3%A9odory" title="Constantin Carath&#233;odory">Carath&#233;odory</a> includes several ideas. The non-deformation state variable of a closed system is represented as a real number. A state of thermal equilibrium between two closed systems connected by a wall permeable only to heat means that a certain mathematical relation holds between the state variables, including the respective non-deformation variables, of those two systems (that particular mathematical relation is regarded by Buchdahl as a preferred statement of the zeroth law of thermodynamics).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Also, referring to thermal contact equilibrium, "whenever each of the systems <span class="texhtml"><i>S</i><sub>1</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>S</i><sub>2</sub></span> is made to reach equilibrium with a third system <span class="texhtml"><i>S</i><sub>3</sub></span> under identical conditions, the systems <span class="texhtml"><i>S</i><sub>1</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>S</i><sub>2</sub></span> are in mutual equilibrium."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carath&#233;odory_1909_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carath&#233;odory_1909-22">[22]</a></sup> It may be viewed as a re-statement of the principle stated by <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">Maxwell</a> in the words: "All heat is of the same kind."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> This physical idea is also expressed by Bailyn as a possible version of the zeroth law of thermodynamics: "All diathermal walls are equivalent."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Thus the present definition of thermodynamic temperature rests on the zeroth law of thermodynamics. Explicitly, this present definition of thermodynamic temperature also rests on the first law of thermodynamics, for the determination of amounts of energy transferred as heat.\n</p><p>Implicitly for this definition, the second law of thermodynamics provides information that establishes the virtuous character of the temperature so defined. It provides that any working substance that complies with the requirement stated in this definition will lead to the same ratio of thermodynamic temperatures, which in this sense is universal, or absolute. The second law of thermodynamics also provides that the thermodynamic temperature defined in this way is positive, because this definition requires that the heat reservoirs not be in thermal equilibrium with one another, and the cycle can be imagined to operate only in one sense if net work is to be supplied to the work reservoir.\n</p><p>Numerical details are settled by making one of the heat reservoirs a cell at the triple point of water, which is defined to have an absolute temperature of 273.16 K.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>  The zeroth law of thermodynamics allows this definition to be used to measure the absolute or thermodynamic temperature of an arbitrary body of interest, by making the other heat reservoir have the same temperature as the body of interest.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intensive_variability">Intensive variability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Intensive variability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In thermodynamic terms, temperature is an <a href="/wiki/Intensive_and_extensive_properties" title="Intensive and extensive properties">intensive variable</a> because it is equal to a <a href="/wiki/Differential_coefficient" title="Differential coefficient">differential coefficient</a> of one <a href="/wiki/Intensive_and_extensive_properties" title="Intensive and extensive properties">extensive variable</a> with respect to another, for a given body. It thus has the <a href="/wiki/Dimensional_analysis" title="Dimensional analysis">dimensions</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">ratio</a> of two extensive variables. In thermodynamics, two bodies are often considered as connected by contact with a common wall, which has some specific permeability properties. Such specific permeability can be referred to a specific intensive variable. An example is a diathermic wall that is permeable only to heat; the intensive variable for this case is temperature. When the two bodies have been in contact for a very long time, and have settled to a permanent steady state, the relevant intensive variables are equal in the two bodies; for a diathermal wall, this statement is sometimes called the zeroth law of thermodynamics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munster_49_69_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Munster_49_69-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailyn_14_214_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bailyn_14_214-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In particular, when the body is described by stating its <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">internal energy</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>U</i></span>, an extensive variable, as a function of its <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>S</i></span>, also an extensive variable, and other state variables <span class="texhtml"><i>V</i>, <i>N</i></span>, with <span class="texhtml"><i>U</i> = <i>U</i> (<i>S</i>, <i>V</i>, <i>N</i></span>), then the temperature is equal to the <a href="/wiki/Partial_derivative" title="Partial derivative">partial derivative</a> of the internal energy with respect to the entropy:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munster_49_69_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-Munster_49_69-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bailyn_14_214_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bailyn_14_214-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Callen_146&#8211;148_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Callen_146&#8211;148-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd>\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse; background:none; margin:0; border:none;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="nowrap" style="vertical-align:middle; border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0;"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle T=\\left({\\frac {\\partial U}{\\partial S}}\\right)_{V,N}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>T</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8706;<!-- &#8706; --></mi>\n<mi>U</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8706;<!-- &#8706; --></mi>\n<mi>S</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>V</mi>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mi>N</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle T=\\left({\\frac {\\partial U}{\\partial S}}\\right)_{V,N}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle T=\\left({\\frac {\\partial U}{\\partial S}}\\right)_{V,N}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cd6f87f64046d2b73b10cfe308a6fbe4fd6c4da1" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:16.152ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></p>\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align:middle; width:99%; border:none; padding:0.08em;">\n<div style="margin:0;">\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse; background:none; margin:0; border:none; width:99%;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td rowspan="2" style="border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0; font-size:4pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td>\n<td style="width:100%; border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0; font-size:1pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0; font-size:4pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="border-left:none; border-top:0px none #e5e5e5; border-right:none; border-bottom:none; padding:0.08em;">\n<p style="margin:0; font-size:1pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n</td>\n<td class="nowrap" style="vertical-align:middle; border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0pt;"><b>(2)</b></p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>\n<p>Likewise, when the body is described by stating its entropy <span class="texhtml"><i>S</i></span> as a function of its internal energy <span class="texhtml"><i>U</i></span>, and other state variables <span class="texhtml"><i>V</i>, <i>N</i></span>, with <span class="texhtml"><i>S</i> = <i>S</i> (<i>U</i>, <i>V</i>, <i>N</i>)</span>, then the reciprocal of the temperature is equal to the partial derivative of the entropy with respect to the internal energy:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Munster_49_69_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-Munster_49_69-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Callen_146&#8211;148_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Callen_146&#8211;148-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<dl><dd>\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse; background:none; margin:0; border:none;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td class="nowrap" style="vertical-align:middle; border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0;"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {1}{T}}=\\left({\\frac {\\partial S}{\\partial U}}\\right)_{V,N}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mi>T</mi>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mrow>\n<mo>(</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8706;<!-- &#8706; --></mi>\n<mi>S</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#8706;<!-- &#8706; --></mi>\n<mi>U</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>V</mi>\n<mo>,</mo>\n<mi>N</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle {\\frac {1}{T}}=\\left({\\frac {\\partial S}{\\partial U}}\\right)_{V,N}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle {\\frac {1}{T}}=\\left({\\frac {\\partial S}{\\partial U}}\\right)_{V,N}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5aa51cb1b5a33b05caaf459ea4036c77ace816f6" style="vertical-align: -2.838ex; width:16.988ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></p>\n</td>\n<td style="vertical-align:middle; width:99%; border:none; padding:0.08em;">\n<div style="margin:0;">\n<table style="border-collapse:collapse; background:none; margin:0; border:none; width:99%;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td rowspan="2" style="border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0; font-size:4pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td>\n<td style="width:100%; border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0; font-size:1pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td>\n<td rowspan="2" style="border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0; font-size:4pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="border-left:none; border-top:0px none #e5e5e5; border-right:none; border-bottom:none; padding:0.08em;">\n<p style="margin:0; font-size:1pt;">&#160;</p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</div>\n</td>\n<td class="nowrap" style="vertical-align:middle; border:none; padding:0.08em;"><p style="margin:0pt;"><b>(3)</b></p>\n</td></tr></tbody></table></dd></dl>\n<p>The above definition, equation (1), of the absolute temperature is due to Kelvin. It refers to systems closed to transfer of matter, and has special emphasis on directly experimental procedures. A presentation of thermodynamics by Gibbs starts at a more abstract level and deals with systems open to the transfer of matter; in this development of thermodynamics, the equations (2) and (3) above are actually alternative definitions of temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_thermodynamic_equilibrium">Local thermodynamic equilibrium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Local thermodynamic equilibrium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Real world bodies are often not in thermodynamic equilibrium and not homogeneous. For study by methods of classical irreversible thermodynamics, a body is usually spatially and temporally divided conceptually into \'cells\' of small size. If classical thermodynamic equilibrium conditions for matter are fulfilled to good approximation in such a \'cell\', then it is homogeneous and a temperature exists for it. If this is so for every \'cell\' of the body, then <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium#Local_and_global_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">local thermodynamic equilibrium</a> is said to prevail throughout the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It makes good sense, for example, to say of the extensive variable <span class="texhtml"><i>U</i></span>, or of the extensive variable <span class="texhtml"><i>S</i></span>, that it has a density per unit volume, or a quantity per unit mass of the system, but it makes no sense to speak of density of temperature per unit volume or quantity of temperature per unit mass of the system. On the other hand, it makes no sense to speak of the internal energy at a point, while when local thermodynamic equilibrium prevails, it makes good sense to speak of the temperature at a point. Consequently, temperature can vary from point to point in a medium that is not in global thermodynamic equilibrium, but in which there is local thermodynamic equilibrium.\n</p><p>Thus, when local thermodynamic equilibrium prevails in a body, temperature can be regarded as a spatially varying local property in that body, and this is because temperature is an intensive variable.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Kinetic_theory_approach">Kinetic theory approach</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Kinetic theory approach">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>A more thorough account of this is below at <a href="#Theoretical_foundation">Theoretical foundation</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kinetic_theory_of_gases" title="Kinetic theory of gases">Kinetic theory</a> provides a microscopic explanation of temperature, based on macroscopic systems\' being composed of many microscopic particles, such as <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecules</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ions</a> of various species, the particles of a species being all alike. It explains macroscopic phenomena through the <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">classical mechanics</a> of the microscopic particles. The <a href="/wiki/Equipartition_theorem" title="Equipartition theorem">equipartition theorem</a> of kinetic theory asserts that each classical <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">degree of freedom</a> of a freely moving particle has an average kinetic energy of <span class="texhtml"><i>k</i><sub>B</sub><i>T</i>/2</span> where <span class="texhtml"><i>k</i><sub>B</sub></span> denotes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boltzmann%27s_constant" title="Boltzmann\'s constant">Boltzmann\'s constant</a>. The translational motion of the particle has three degrees of freedom, so that, except at very low temperatures where quantum effects predominate, the average translational kinetic energy of a freely moving particle in a system with temperature <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i></span> will be <span class="texhtml">3<i>k</i><sub>B</sub><i>T</i>/2</span>.\n</p><p>It is possible to measure the average kinetic energy of constituent microscopic particles if they are allowed to escape from the bulk of the system. The spectrum of velocities has to be measured, and the average calculated from that. It is not necessarily the case that the particles that escape and are measured have the same velocity distribution as the particles that remain in the bulk of the system, but sometimes a good sample is possible.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">Molecules</a>, such as oxygen (O<sub>2</sub>), have more <a href="/wiki/Degrees_of_freedom_(physics_and_chemistry)" title="Degrees of freedom (physics and chemistry)">degrees of freedom</a> than single spherical atoms: they undergo rotational and vibrational motions as well as translations. Heating results in an increase in temperature due to an increase in the average translational kinetic energy of the molecules. Heating will also cause, through <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equipartition" title="Equipartition">equipartitioning</a>, the energy associated with vibrational and rotational modes to increase. Thus a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diatomic" title="Diatomic">diatomic</a> gas will require more energy input to increase its temperature by a certain amount, i.e. it will have a greater <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">heat capacity</a> than a monatomic gas.\n</p><p>The process of cooling involves removing internal energy from a system. When no more energy can be removed, the system is at absolute zero, though this cannot be achieved experimentally. Absolute zero is the null point of the <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">thermodynamic temperature</a> scale, also called absolute temperature. If it were possible to cool a system to absolute zero, all classical motion of its particles would cease and they would be at complete rest in this classical sense. Microscopically in the description of quantum mechanics, however, matter still has <a href="/wiki/Zero-point_energy" title="Zero-point energy">zero-point energy</a> even at absolute zero, because of the <a href="/wiki/Uncertainty_principle" title="Uncertainty principle">uncertainty principle</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_theory">Basic theory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Basic theory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="navbox" style="width: 18em; margin-left: 1em; float: right;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Conjugate_variables_(thermodynamics)" title="Conjugate variables (thermodynamics)">Conjugate variables<br/>of thermodynamics</a>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">Pressure</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Volume_(thermodynamics)" title="Volume (thermodynamics)">Volume</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>(<a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">Stress</a>)</td>\n<td>(<a href="/wiki/Deformation_(mechanics)#Strain" title="Deformation (mechanics)">Strain</a>)\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Temperature</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">Entropy</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Chemical_potential" title="Chemical potential">Chemical potential</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Particle_number" title="Particle number">Particle number</a>\n</td></tr></tbody></table><p>Temperature is a measure of a <a href="/wiki/Categories_(Aristotle)#The_Praedicamenta" title="Categories (Aristotle)">quality</a> of a state of a material.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>  The quality may be regarded as a more abstract entity than any particular temperature scale that measures it, and is called <i>hotness</i> by some writers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The quality of hotness refers to the state of material only in a particular locality, and in general, apart from bodies held in a steady state of thermodynamic equilibrium, hotness varies from place to place. It is not necessarily the case that a material in a particular place is in a state that is steady and nearly homogeneous enough to allow it to have a well-defined hotness or temperature. Hotness may be represented abstractly as a one-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Manifold" title="Manifold">manifold</a>. Every valid temperature scale has its own one-to-one map into the hotness manifold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mach_1900_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_1900-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serrin_1986_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Serrin_1986-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When two systems in thermal contact are at the same temperature no heat transfers between them. When a temperature difference does exist heat flows spontaneously from the warmer system to the colder system until they are in <a href="/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium" title="Thermal equilibrium">thermal equilibrium</a>. Heat transfer occurs by conduction or by thermal radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxwell_1872_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell_1872-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tait_1884_39-40_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tait_1884_39-40-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck_1897_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planck_1897-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dugdale_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-dugdale-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reif_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-reif-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moran_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moran-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Experimental physicists, for example <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei#Technology" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newtons_law_of_cooling" title="Newtons law of cooling">Newton</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> found that there are indefinitely many <a href="/wiki/Scale_of_temperature" title="Scale of temperature">empirical temperature scales</a>. Nevertheless, the <a href="/wiki/Zeroth_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Zeroth law of thermodynamics">zeroth law of thermodynamics</a> says that they all measure the same quality.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bodies_in_thermodynamic_equilibrium">Bodies in thermodynamic equilibrium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Bodies in thermodynamic equilibrium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For experimental physics, hotness means that, when comparing any two given bodies in their respective separate <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">thermodynamic equilibria</a>, any two suitably given empirical thermometers with numerical scale readings will agree as to which is the hotter of the two given bodies, or that they have the same temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> This does not require the two thermometers to have a linear relation between their numerical scale readings, but it does require that the relation between their numerical readings shall be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotonic" title="Monotonic">strictly monotonic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> A definite sense of greater hotness can be had, independently of <a href="/wiki/Calorimetry" title="Calorimetry">calorimetry</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamics" title="Thermodynamics">thermodynamics</a>, and of properties of particular materials, from <a href="/wiki/Wien%27s_displacement_law#Frequency-dependent_formulation" title="Wien\'s displacement law">Wien\'s displacement law</a> of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal radiation</a>: the temperature of a bath of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">thermal radiation</a> is <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">proportional</a>, by a universal constant, to the frequency of the maximum of its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frequency_spectrum#Light" title="Frequency spectrum">frequency spectrum</a>; this frequency is always positive, but can have values that <a href="/wiki/Third_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Third law of thermodynamics">tend to zero</a>. Thermal radiation is initially defined for a cavity in thermodynamic equilibrium. These physical facts justify a mathematical statement that hotness exists on an ordered one-dimensional <a href="/wiki/Manifold" title="Manifold">manifold</a>. This is a fundamental character of temperature and thermometers for bodies in their own thermodynamic equilibrium.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truesdell_1980_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Truesdell_1980-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mach_1900_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_1900-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serrin_1986_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Serrin_1986-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitteri_1984_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pitteri_1984-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Except for a system undergoing a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Order_parameter" title="Order parameter">first-order</a> <a href="/wiki/Phase_transition" title="Phase transition">phase change</a> such as the melting of ice, as a closed system receives heat, without change in its volume and without change in external force fields acting on it, its temperature rises. For a system undergoing such a phase change so slowly that departure from thermodynamic equilibrium can be neglected, its temperature remains constant as the system is supplied with <a href="/wiki/Latent_heat" title="Latent heat">latent heat</a>. Conversely, a loss of heat from a closed system, without phase change, without change of volume, and without change in external force fields acting on it, decreases its temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bodies_in_a_steady_state_but_not_in_thermodynamic_equilibrium">Bodies in a steady state but not in thermodynamic equilibrium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Bodies in a steady state but not in thermodynamic equilibrium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>While for bodies in their own thermodynamic equilibrium states, the notion of temperature requires that all empirical thermometers must agree as to which of two bodies is the hotter or that they are at the same temperature, this requirement is not safe for bodies that are in steady states though not in thermodynamic equilibrium. It can then well be that different empirical thermometers disagree about which is the hotter, and if this is so, then at least one of the bodies does not have a well defined absolute thermodynamic temperature. Nevertheless, any one given body and any one suitable empirical thermometer can still support notions of empirical, non-absolute, hotness and temperature, for a suitable range of processes. This is a matter for study in <a href="/wiki/Non-equilibrium_thermodynamics" title="Non-equilibrium thermodynamics">non-equilibrium thermodynamics</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bodies_not_in_a_steady_state">Bodies not in a steady state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Bodies not in a steady state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>When a body is not in a steady state, then the notion of temperature becomes even less safe than for a body in a steady state not in thermodynamic equilibrium. This is also a matter for study in <a href="/wiki/Non-equilibrium_thermodynamics" title="Non-equilibrium thermodynamics">non-equilibrium thermodynamics</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermodynamic_equilibrium_axiomatics">Thermodynamic equilibrium axiomatics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Thermodynamic equilibrium axiomatics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>For axiomatic treatment of thermodynamic equilibrium, since the 1930s, it has become customary to refer to a <a href="/wiki/Zeroth_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Zeroth law of thermodynamics">zeroth law of thermodynamics</a>. The customarily stated minimalist version of such a law postulates only that all bodies, which when thermally connected would be in thermal equilibrium, should be said to have the same temperature by definition, but by itself does not establish temperature as a quantity expressed as a real number on a scale. A more physically informative version of such a law views empirical temperature as a chart on a hotness manifold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mach_1900_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_1900-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pitteri_1984_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pitteri_1984-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Serrin_1978_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Serrin_1978-56">[56]</a></sup> While the zeroth law permits the definitions of many different empirical scales of temperature, the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a> selects the definition of a single preferred, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_temperature" title="Absolute temperature">absolute temperature</a>, unique up to an arbitrary scale factor, whence called the <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_temperature" title="Thermodynamic temperature">thermodynamic temperature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truesdell_1980_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-Truesdell_1980-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mach_1900_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mach_1900-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maxwell_1872_155-158_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Maxwell_1872_155-158-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tait_1884_68-69_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tait_1884_68-69-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> If <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">internal energy</a> is considered as a function of the volume and entropy of a homogeneous system in thermodynamic equilibrium, thermodynamic absolute temperature appears as the partial derivative of <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">internal energy</a> with respect the <a href="/wiki/Entropy" title="Entropy">entropy</a> at constant volume. Its natural, intrinsic origin or null point is <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a> at which the entropy of any system is at a minimum. Although this is the lowest absolute temperature described by the model, the <a href="/wiki/Third_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Third law of thermodynamics">third law of thermodynamics</a> postulates that absolute zero cannot be attained by any physical system.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Heat_capacity">Heat capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Heat capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">Heat capacity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calorimetry" title="Calorimetry">Calorimetry</a></div>\n<p>When an energy transfer to or from a body is only as heat, the state of the body changes. Depending on the surroundings and the walls separating them from the body, various changes are possible in the body. They include chemical reactions, increase of pressure, increase of temperature, and phase change. For each kind of change under specified conditions, the heat capacity is the ratio of the quantity of heat transferred to the magnitude of the change. For example, if the change is an increase in temperature at constant volume, with no phase change and no chemical change, then the temperature of the body rises and its pressure increases. The quantity of heat transferred, <span class="texhtml">&#916;<i>Q</i></span>, divided by the observed temperature change, <span class="texhtml">&#916;<i>T</i></span>, is the body\'s <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">heat capacity</a> at constant volume:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle C_{V}={\\frac {\\Delta Q}{\\Delta T}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>C</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>V</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>Q</mi>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi mathvariant="normal">&#916;<!-- &#916; --></mi>\n<mi>T</mi>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle C_{V}={\\frac {\\Delta Q}{\\Delta T}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle C_{V}={\\frac {\\Delta Q}{\\Delta T}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6adea171556775a098ac428a17d8fcc7f082f7d0" style="vertical-align: -2.005ex; width:11.513ex; height:5.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>If heat capacity is measured for a well defined <a href="/wiki/Amount_of_substance" title="Amount of substance">amount of substance</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Specific_heat" title="Specific heat">specific heat</a> is the measure of the heat required to increase the temperature of such a unit quantity by one unit of temperature. For example, to raise the temperature of water by one kelvin (equal to one degree Celsius) requires 4186 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Joules" title="Joules">joules</a> per <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">kilogram</a> (J/kg).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">Measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pakkanen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2269" data-file-width="1140" decoding="async" height="438" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Pakkanen.jpg/220px-Pakkanen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Pakkanen.jpg/330px-Pakkanen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Pakkanen.jpg/440px-Pakkanen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pakkanen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical Celsius thermometer measures a winter day temperature of <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002256149999999999&#9824;</span>&#8722;17&#160;&#176;C</span></div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_temperature_and_pressure_measurement_technology" title="Timeline of temperature and pressure measurement technology">Timeline of temperature and pressure measurement technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/International_Temperature_Scale_of_1990" title="International Temperature Scale of 1990">International Temperature Scale of 1990</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comparison_of_temperature_scales" title="Comparison of temperature scales">Comparison of temperature scales</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Temperature_measurement" title="Temperature measurement">Temperature measurement</a> using modern scientific <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">thermometers</a> and temperature scales goes back at least as far as the early 18th century, when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gabriel_Fahrenheit" title="Gabriel Fahrenheit">Gabriel Fahrenheit</a> adapted a thermometer (switching to <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>) and a scale both developed by <a href="/wiki/Ole_R%C3%B8mer" title="Ole R&#248;mer">Ole Christensen R&#248;mer</a>. Fahrenheit\'s scale is still in use in the United States for non-scientific applications.\n</p><p>Temperature is measured with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermometers" title="Thermometers">thermometers</a> that may be <a href="/wiki/Calibration" title="Calibration">calibrated</a> to a variety of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperature_conversion_formulas" title="Temperature conversion formulas">temperature scales</a>. In most of the world (except for <a href="/wiki/Belize" title="Belize">Belize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>), the Celsius scale is used for most temperature measuring purposes. Most scientists measure temperature using the Celsius scale and thermodynamic temperature using the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">Kelvin</a> scale, which is the Celsius scale offset so that its null point is <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>0&#160;K</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>&#8722;273.15&#160;&#176;C</span>, or <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a>. Many engineering fields in the US, notably high-tech and US federal specifications (civil and military), also use the Kelvin and Celsius scales. Other engineering fields in the US also rely upon the <a href="/wiki/Rankine_scale" title="Rankine scale">Rankine scale</a> (a shifted Fahrenheit scale) when working in thermodynamic-related disciplines such as <a href="/wiki/Combustion" title="Combustion">combustion</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Units">Units</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Units">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The basic unit of temperature in the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> (SI) is the <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a>. It has the symbol K.\n</p><p>For everyday applications, it is often convenient to use the Celsius scale, in which <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273149999999999&#9824;</span>0&#160;&#176;C</span> corresponds very closely to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freezing_point" title="Freezing point">freezing point</a> of water and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002373150000000000&#9824;</span>100&#160;&#176;C</span> is its <a href="/wiki/Boiling_point" title="Boiling point">boiling point</a> at sea level. Because liquid droplets commonly exist in clouds at sub-zero temperatures, <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273149999999999&#9824;</span>0&#160;&#176;C</span> is better defined as the melting point of ice. In this scale a temperature difference of 1 degree Celsius is the same as a <span style="white-space:nowrap">1<span style="margin-left:0.25em">kelvin</span></span> increment, but the scale is offset by the temperature at which ice melts (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273149999999999&#9824;</span>273.15&#160;K</span>).\n</p><p>By international agreement<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> the Kelvin and Celsius scales are defined by two fixing points: <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vienna_Standard_Mean_Ocean_Water" title="Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water">Vienna Standard Mean Ocean Water</a>, which is water specially prepared with a specified blend of hydrogen and oxygen isotopes. Absolute zero is defined as precisely <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>0&#160;K</span> and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>&#8722;273.15&#160;&#176;C</span>. It is the temperature at which all classical translational motion of the particles comprising matter ceases and they are at complete rest in the classical model. Quantum-mechanically, however, zero-point motion remains and has an associated energy, the <a href="/wiki/Zero-point_energy" title="Zero-point energy">zero-point energy</a>.  Matter is in its <a href="/wiki/Ground_state" title="Ground state">ground state</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and contains no <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a>. The triple point of water is defined as <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273160000000000&#9824;</span>273.16&#160;K</span> and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273159999999999&#9824;</span>0.01&#160;&#176;C</span>. This definition serves the following purposes: it fixes the magnitude of the kelvin as being precisely 1 part in 273.16 parts of the difference between absolute zero and the triple point of water; it establishes that one kelvin has precisely the same magnitude as one degree on the Celsius scale; and it establishes the difference between the null points of these scales as being <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273149999999999&#9824;</span>273.15&#160;K</span> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>0&#160;K</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">5000000000000000000&#9824;</span>&#8722;273.15&#160;&#176;C</span> and <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273160000000000&#9824;</span>273.16&#160;K</span> = <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273159999999999&#9824;</span>0.01&#160;&#176;C</span>).\n</p><p>In the United States, the Fahrenheit scale is widely used. On this scale the freezing point of water corresponds to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002273149999999999&#9824;</span>32&#160;&#176;F</span> and the boiling point to <span class="nowrap"><span class="sortkey" style="display:none">7002373150000000000&#9824;</span>212&#160;&#176;F</span>. The Rankine scale, still used in fields of chemical engineering in the US, is an absolute scale based on the Fahrenheit increment.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion">Conversion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Temperature&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Conversion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The following table shows the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperature_conversion_formulas" title="Temperature conversion formulas">temperature conversion formulas</a> for conversions to and from the Celsius scale.\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<caption>Temperature conversions\n</caption>\n<tbody><tr>\n<th nowrap"><sup>5</sup>&#8260;<sub>9</sub></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th height="30pt">Delisle\n</th>\n<td>[&#176;De]&#160;=&#160;(100&#160;&#8722;&#160;[&#176;C])&#160;&#215;&#160;&#8203;<span nowrap"><sup>2</sup>&#8260;<sub>3</sub></span>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<th height="30pt">Newton\n</th>\n<td>[&#176;N]&#160;=&#160;[&#176;C]&#160;&#215;&#160;&#8203;<span class="frac
